## 721

B"H, 7 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

...And since everything is by Divine Providence—especially according to what is explained in the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov, as this year is the bicentennial of his passing and hillula—it is my duty (and privilege) to arouse you, and through you all those on whose behalf you write, regarding the words of our Rebbes, the Nesi'im [beginning with our master the Baal Shem Tov, and his successor the Maggid, and our Alter Rebbe, author of the Tanya (the authority on the hidden aspects of Torah) and the Shulchan Aruch (the authority on the revealed aspects of Torah)], concerning the immense necessity of studying the inner dimensions of Torah, which in our generation has been revealed in the teachings of Chassidus. They sacrificed their lives for the dissemination of this study. If this applies to everyone, it applies all the more so to those who can influence others, as per the teaching of the Mishnah, "He was meritorious and caused the many to be meritorious," and from the positive we can infer the negative, etc. Especially in this period of *ikvesa d'meshicha*, when "behold, he is standing behind our wall," and everyone must be ready every day for his arrival, it is imperative that each person do everything that depends on them. As per the words of Melech HaMashiach, as recounted in the Iggeres HaKodesh of the Baal Shem Tov, that when his wellsprings spread outward, he will come. Heaven forbid, heaven forbid, to prolong the exile even for the briefest moment through a lack of action in the aforementioned dissemination, or even through action that is not complete—for this is the exile of the Ribono shel Olam and the exile of Knesses Yisrael, who went into exile to Edom, and the Shechinah is with them. It is difficult to elaborate on a matter that is distressing to the point of alarm.

And as we find ourselves in the month of Elul, the month of accounting and mercy, when "the King is in the field," as explained in Likutei Torah on the maamar beginning "Ani L'dodi" (Parshas Re'eh), surely everyone is being aroused from Above regarding the aforementioned. May it be the will that there be an *is'arusa d'leila* that leads to an *is'arusa d'לתתא*, in a manner of "increasing and continuing, increasing and illuminating." May it be the will that I receive good news regarding this—and there is no "good" other than actual deed, for the deed is the essential thing, and there is nothing that can stand before the will.

With blessings for good tidings and to be inscribed and sealed for good.

#### ***721***

Printed in Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 20, p. 589.

## 722

B.H., 8 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.

The Bicentennial Year of the Passing (Hilula)
of the Baal Shem Tov, of blessed memory.

To the distinguished Rabbi, the Chassid, the G-d-fearing man, who is involved in communal affairs, etc.,
Rabbi R. Sh. Y., may he live and be well.

Greetings and Blessings,

I was pleased to receive the notification from your honor that on Monday, Parshas Ki Savo, you will, with the help of Hashem, complete the study of the Shas. Surely, in accordance with the Jewish custom regarding Torah, you will immediately begin studying it anew, for "its end is embedded in its beginning, and its beginning in its end."

This is especially true when studying in a group, for the very act of communal study connects all participants—"from your heads, your tribes, to your woodchoppers and water-drawers"—until they become unified as one. This is as our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe explains regarding the verse, "You are standing today, all of you," a parsha that pertains to the days preceding Rosh Hashanah.

Through this unity, the "woodchoppers and water-drawers" are elevated, as are the "heads of your tribes," and so on, as explained in the aforementioned discourse.

One should also integrate and connect the above with the completion and commencement of the Shas.

The Gemara states at the end of the Shas: "Rav Shemaya said to R. Abba, 'Tell me, if she is a *zavah* during the day, will she be a *niddah* at night?' He replied, 'Go, the verse says, "concerning her *niddah*," close to her *niddah*. When is it close to her *niddah*? At night, and yet it calls her a *zavah*.'" Following this, the text mentions the *Tanna d’Bei Eliyahu*, etc.—though some manuscripts omit this. Even for those who include it, it is there to conclude with words of praise and consolation, as an addition.

In the context of *avodah*, this aligns with the adage of our teacher, the Baal Shem Tov, that every matter or event a person sees or hears contains a lesson for his *avodah* of his Creator. This is all the more true regarding our Torah, the "Torah of life," which is exactly as its name implies—a guide for life.

The distinction between the impurity of a *niddah* and a *zavah* is that the former is part of the nature of the world—albeit only after the sin—whereas *zivah* is a sickness of the body and is not natural.

Similarly, in *avodah*, there is the matter of impurity (the source of which is that which is called "impure"), which comes by nature, though only after the sin. This is to such an extent that even the four who died only because of the "counsel of the serpent"—the primordial sin—are included.

In terms of levels closer to us, this is understood through the defense offered by the Maggid of Mezritch regarding the thoughts of the Baal Shem Tov’s disciples. They wondered why he would engage with simple people through such lofty and sublime acts of drawing them close. The Maggid performed several *tikkunim* to rectify this line of thought, yet he was not at ease until one night, while walking through the chambers of Gan Eden, he passed through a chamber where children were studying Chumash. Moshe Rabbeinu was explaining to them the verse, "And Avraham fell on his face," and Yitzchak, noting that a verse never loses its literal meaning. If you ask how it is possible that Avraham could be in doubt regarding Hashem’s word, know that this is from the perspective of the body—and even a holy body is flesh.

There is that which incites the *yetzer hara* against a person—this **sickness**, the *yetzer hara*, is the impurity of *zivah*.

And Rav Shemaya (representing *havanah* and *hasagah*—understanding and comprehension) said to R. Abba (representing the **awe** of a son for his father, rather than the understanding of a father’s command; *Abba* being the level of *Chochma*, which is above *havanah* and *hasagah*): "Tell me, during the day (when the 'sun and shield' of Hashem Elokim shines, and yet one sees blood emerging and separating—vitality and warmth in external matters), will she be a *zavah* (for this is the *yetzer hara* inciting until one becomes like 'wine in the heat of Tammuz'—cool)?" "At night (in the time of darkness, 'I will surely hide My face'—even though one sees blood), will she be a *niddah* (a natural matter, for it comes through the concealment)?"

He said to him (R. Abba, who is above *hasagah* and *havanah*, and has the ability to resolve the difficulty—even though the difficulty is logically correct from the perspective of the intellect, the Oral Torah, whose primary focus is understanding): "Upon you (above you, above *havanah* and *hasagah*), the verse says (the Written Torah, whose essence is the letters and verses, not the understanding—which is why even an unlearned person recites the blessing over the Torah), 'concerning her *niddah*, close to her *niddah*' (every emission of blood **outward**, whatever it may be, is possible only because of the proximity—as a result of the first sin which acted upon nature and cast impurity into it), 'close to her *niddah*—when is it? At night (the proximity and the result of *niddah*—night, the concealment of the light of the sun).' And yet it calls her (the Written Torah, the Scripture, which calls to Hashem) a *zavah* (implying that this is not necessary, but is merely a sickness, for Hashem helps her, specifically through the level of *Chochma* in the soul—the *mesiras nefesh* that exists in everyone, so that even at night his candle will not be extinguished, 'the candle of Hashem is the soul of man,' and Hashem will illuminate his darkness)."

And "its end is embedded in its beginning," that in the darkness of the night, the command is given (which is also the granting of strength, for Hashem does not deal harshly with His creatures) to recite the Shema in the evening, and to crown Him above, below, and to the four directions. The power for this is **"From when?"**—and as our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe explains in the name of the angel: *Me'eimasa*—from *eimah* (awe) and fear, the awe of majesty, *yirah ila'ah* (the level of *Chochma*), and down to the lowest level—the fear of punishment, *yirah tata'ah*, "from your heads, your tribes, to your woodchoppers and water-drawers."

May it be the will of Hashem that despite the night of the *galus*, a double and redoubled darkness, each one will fulfill the recitation (and drawing down) of the Shema, etc., "One" (see Zohar Vol. II, 115b, that the recitation of Shema cannot be redeemed by another, and the later authorities have elaborated on this), until the darkness is turned into light and the night shines like the day.

With respect and with the blessing of *Kesivah V’Chasimah Tovah* to each of the participants in the aforementioned study, may they live and be well.

## 7,323

B"H, 9 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

...As we stand in the month of Elul, the month of mercy—whose acronym is "and righteousness shall be ours, for..." (Devarim 6:25; Mishnas Chassidim, tractate Elul, at the beginning)—may it be the will of Hashem that our avodah of Hashem be as the holy Ohr HaChaim explains the verse: avodah out of love, and the ultimate purpose of love is not for the sake of any ulterior motive. See *Kuntres HaAvodah*, chapter 5, that love which is not dependent on anything, because it stems from the yechidah, acts even within the realm of asiyah, as the verse concludes: "to do."

And just as "as water reflects a face to a face," so too will it be drawn down from Above, along the line of love and chesed, where the influence is without boundaries or tzimtzumim, reaching down to the lowest levels, in both the physical and the spiritual alike.

## 724

724

## 7225

B"H, 10 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the end of the month of Menachem Av, in which you write regarding a *sheitel* (wig), and that in the religious environment where you are located, this is not the custom, and you are embarrassed, perhaps they will laugh at you if you wear a *sheitel*.

Regarding the essence of the matter of wearing a *sheitel*—and not being satisfied with a hat or a *tichel* (headscarf)—the necessity for this is explained in several places. For it is seen tangibly that wearing a hat, and even a *tichel*, leaves a portion of the hair uncovered, at the very least for a **short** time—meaning that one transgresses the great prohibition, as per the ruling in *Shulchan Aruch*, *Orach Chaim*, section 75. The magnitude of the matter is also understood from the magnitude of the reward for fulfilling the commandment as we have been commanded, and in the language of the holy *Zohar*, one is blessed with everything: with blessings from above and blessings from below, with wealth, with children, and with children’s children.

And regarding your writing that perhaps they will laugh at you and you will be embarrassed, etc.:

Even among the youth in the United States, there has recently been a sense of respect felt precisely for those who stand firm in their convictions and are not embarrassed by those who mock them and their worldview. They relate with mockery and disdain to those who are drawn after the majority without any inner stability at all. And surely you know that the beginning of all four parts of the *Shulchan Aruch* is: "And one should not be embarrassed before people who mock him in the service of Hashem Yisbarach."

Furthermore, and this is also understood and simple: Hashem Yisbarach, "I fill the heavens and the earth," and is found with a person in **every** place and at **every** time—which is not the case with people, even from the closest environment, who are not always in close proximity to one. Therefore, is it possible that one would not be embarrassed, *chas v'shalom*, before the *Ribono shel Olam*, yet be embarrassed before a human being of flesh and blood?!

My hope is that, consistent with the conclusion of your letter—from your upbringing—there is no need for further length regarding what has been said.

And Hashem Yisbarach, who oversees every single person with *hashgacha pratis*—a matter which is a foundation of our faith, as explained at length in the teachings of our master, the Baal Shem Tov, *z"l*, whose *hilula* marks two hundred years since his passing this year—will guide you well, both materially and spiritually.

With blessings for good tidings and for a *kesivah v’chasimah tovah*.

On behalf of the Rebbe, *shlita*,
Secretary

## 726

B"H, 12 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

Following the longest of interruptions, your letter of 6 Elul was received.

Regarding what you wrote concerning the well-known matter—the advice for this is to completely divert your attention from it, at least until after the wedding, in a good and auspicious hour. This diversion of attention is not a matter of waging war against negative thoughts, but rather of shifting your focus to something else. It is best to shift to matters of Torah and tefillah, for if a little light pushes away much darkness, then certainly a great deal of light does so all the more. Therefore, you should know by heart at least a few chapters of Mishnayos and a few chapters of Tanya, and review them in your thoughts or even aloud whenever you have the opportunity.

It is also appropriate that every weekday, before morning tefillah, you set aside a few coins for tzedakah, and that you keep up with the three well-known daily shiurim: Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya.

Regarding what you wrote about fasting, you should replace this with a "taanis ratzon"—meaning, do not indulge your own will by eating a favorite food or the like, and instead replace these with the pleasantness and sweetness of Torah and its mitzvos. Through this, it is understood—and it is also simple—that you will have an influence on others. You must view yourself as a shaliach of the Makom, B"H, as the Mishnah states at the end of Kiddushin, "I was created to serve my Maker." Serving one's Maker is accomplished through the dissemination of our Torah, the Torah of life, and the fulfillment of its mitzvos, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them." You must do this wherever your hand can reach, time and again, and with words that come from the heart, for then they enter the heart of the listener. If, for the time being, they have not entered, it is proof that you are not speaking with the necessary sincerity. On the contrary, if you speak as mentioned above, in ways of pleasantness but with strength, not only will you succeed regarding the other person, but you will succeed even more regarding yourself and the struggles of your own soul. These days are an eis ratzon, when (as explained in Likutei Torah by our Rebbe HaZaken, Parshas Re'eh, in the maamar beginning "Ani L'dodi"), the King is in the field, etc., and He receives everyone with a welcoming countenance, etc.

With blessings for good tidings and to be inscribed and sealed for good.

Regarding your question—you should say "Aleinu" with the tzibbur, and "L'Dovid... Ori" afterward.

## 7228

7228

B"H, 15 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I have received your letter dated the 10th of the month of Elul, the month of mercy. Regarding the content of your letter—I am responding immediately, without waiting for my turn. My intention, of course, is the point of *hafatzas hamaayanos chutzah* (spreading the wellsprings of Chassidus outward). I am hurrying in order to utilize the time and the few remaining days of this auspicious year—the bicentennial of the *hillula* of our teacher, the Baal Shem Tov. Your response to what I wrote is, in the style of our Sages, like one who claims wheat and is answered with barley.

What I wrote—and I am both astonished and pained by this—is that you are not utilizing your talents and the ability at your disposal for *hafatzas hamaayanos*. Your response to me—that so-and-so is not in order, and so-and-so is not assisting you, etc.—is simply not relevant to the claim. And even if you were to argue that it is—on the contrary, this only strengthens my point. For if, as you say, many are not in order, this only increases the measure of the *avodah* that rests upon the shoulders of all those who *are* supposed to be in order. My hope and confidence is that you are certainly among them. To state the matter simply, there is surely no need for length, just as there is surely no need to show you the platform you can use for the aforementioned, since several of your *maamarim* and writings have already been printed in various places and also independently.

And although, on the face of it, we are already approaching the end of the aforementioned year, beyond the main point—that even in the two-hundred-and-first year, the command for *hafatzas hamaayanos* remains in full force, even if it is not a jubilee date—there are still many days left in this year itself, and many hours and moments. And in one hour and in one moment, one can accomplish great and hidden things, provided one brings into actuality all the powers and treasures that are within the soul of a Jew. In the language of our Alter Rebbe, this is when there is revealed not only the illumination of the point of the heart, but the very essence of the point of the heart (*Likutei Torah*, *d'h* "Ki Seitzei," section 3). And the beginning of all parts of the *Tur*, part *Orach Chaim*, is: "Be bold as a leopard... to do the will of your Father in Heaven."

May it be His will that I receive good news regarding all that has been said, and as mentioned—this letter is being sent as a priority, urgent dispatch.

With respect and blessing for good tidings and to be inscribed and sealed for good.

## 729

B"H, Motzaei Shabbos, 18 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Birthday of the Baal Shem Tov and the Alter Rebbe

To the avreich Chaim, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of the 6th of Elul, the month of mercy, in which you write regarding the order of conduct during these days of mercy.

Now, although it is stated in the words of the Alter Rebbe that "the King is in the field and receives everyone with a pleasant countenance," etc. (Likutei Torah, Parshas Re'eh, discourse beginning "Ani L'dodi"), it is understood and also simple that one must increase in matters of tefillah, Torah, and mitzvos. This follows the order written, "Ani L'dodi" (first) "v'dodi li," the acronym for Elul, as explained in the books of Kabbalah, mussar, and Chassidus. The general order for this, as brought in the later authorities, is to increase in tefillah and supplications compared to the rest of the year (see Birkei Yosef, cited in Mateh Ephraim, sec. 581, subsec. 11*). Likewise, regarding the study of Torah, one should increase in the portions pertaining to the avodah of Hashem and be meticulous in the performance of His mitzvos. In the seforim of Chabad, there are specific texts for this: Iggeres HaTeshuvah (part 3 of the Tanya), Derech Chaim (by our Rebbe, the Mitteler Rebbe), etc.

May it be the will that you act upon all that has been said—"ya'aseh" (he shall do) implies action—specifically regarding tzedakah, doing so more than is your custom, and more than your intellect dictates, for we have been commanded, "Nullify your will before His will," etc.

With blessings for the study of Torah with yiras shamayim, and for a kesivah v'chasimah tovah.

#### ***729***

*) In Mateh Ephraim, ibid.

## 731

B"H, 20 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn

Blessings and Peace!

I was pleased to receive your letter of 15 Elul, in which you write regarding your holy work at the Oholei Yosef Yitzchak Lubavitch school.

At an auspicious time, you will be mentioned at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in whose name these schools are named.

Regarding your letter concerning the proposal that the teachers remain at their school in order to lead the students to Tashlich, as opposed to the logic of being at home and in the environment to which they are accustomed:

Since the essence of Rosh Hashanah is *kabbalas ol* (accepting the yoke of Heaven), one should accept the aforementioned proposal—at least those teachers who have no special reason for refusal, aside from the aforementioned. They will surely utilize these days to instill the concept of *kabbalas ol* in those they influence and in their environment, and this serves as a channel and vessel for receiving a *kesivah v'chasimah tovah* for a good year, both materially and spiritually. In exchange for this, it is possible for them to be at home on the holy day, or at least for part of the days of our festival of joy, or even all of them, as they will arrange with the principal. If there is room and it is possible to create a rotation among the teachers regarding all of the above, that should be done.

With blessings for success in your work and for a *kesivah v'chasimah tovah*,

**M. Schneerson**

P.S.

Regarding your question at the beginning of your letter, it is understood and simple that your view is correct: to seek a match with someone with whom you will build your home as a Chassidishe home, based on the study of the teachings of Chassidus and conduct according to its guidance and ways. As is known, the great emphasis placed on this in the words and *sichos* of our Rebbes, of blessed memory, is well-known. It is understood that this is especially relevant to the foundation of a home and the building of a home in Israel, an eternal edifice.

## 7,332

B"H, 21 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 17 Elul, in which you write about your daughter, may she live, and how she was raised in a certain home, etc., and the educators are asking you to make a clear decision regarding the situation, presenting two alternatives, etc.

In my opinion, since you write that your daughter is already sixteen years old, you must in any case find an opportune moment to tell her about the entire matter. It is almost certain that she will eventually find out about this, and therefore it is better that she learns of it from those closest to her, at the appropriate time and with the appropriate words.

Since the main point is ultimately the well-being of your daughter, may she live, it is understood and simple that matters must be arranged in such a way that she is in a religious environment and receives religious influence to the greatest extent possible. Taking initiative in this is necessary and good, and perhaps even essential during the formative years of the age mentioned.

Regarding the concern that telling her suddenly might affect her, one can certainly tell her with the appropriate preparations. Furthermore, and most importantly, as mentioned above, it is almost certain that she will find out about this anyway; therefore, it is better that it comes from you yourselves. This is especially true since cases similar to this are not rare (even though, naturally, those involved do not publicize such matters).

Regarding how to practically approach all of the above, it is understood that this depends on several details and the temperament of those involved. You should consult with friends on the spot, and Hashem, who oversees each and every person with hashgacha pratis, will guide each of them toward the true good.

It is understood and simple that an increase in matters of Torah and mitzvos adds to the blessing of Hashem, especially when one is in need of an increase in blessing.

It is appropriate that you have your tefillin checked, as well as the mezuzos in your home, to ensure they are all kosher according to law.

Regarding your request for a blessing: when you write the names along with the mother's name (as is the custom when mentioning someone in prayer), they will be mentioned at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

With blessings for good tidings and to be inscribed and sealed for good.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 733

B"H, 21 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 14 Elul, the month of mercy, in which you write of what you have endured, and recently of the loss you have suffered in the passing of your son... of blessed memory.

May it be the will of the One who comforts Zion and Jerusalem to strengthen each and every one of you as is necessary, and may only goodness and kindness pursue you in the days to come.

In light of what you wrote regarding your family and your background, I trust it is unnecessary to elaborate on the eternity of the soul. Especially since even the sages of the nations acknowledge this without any doubt; the soul merely transitions from one state to another. Therefore, all those who were previously connected to the soul and desire its well-being—and indeed, even when the soul is in the body, the connection and love are directed toward the soul, for the body is but an external garment—must strive to provide *nachas ruach* to the soul of the departed in every way within their power. This is achieved by introducing a spirit of purity and clear light into one's own four cubits and into one's surroundings. This prepares the person and the place to receive the influence of Hashem, who is the true source of life and the ultimate reality. Therefore, in every matter and thing connected to the Source of Life, the verse is fulfilled: "And you who cleave to Hashem your G-d are all alive today." It is certainly unnecessary to emphasize that this refers to our Torah, the Torah of life, and its *mitzvos*, regarding which it is said, "and live by them."

Furthermore, regarding this same matter, though it is surely unnecessary to mention, I trust that everything is being arranged properly concerning the recitation of *Kaddish*, the study of *Mishnayos* in memory of the soul of the departed, and—most importantly—the giving of *tzedakah*, both in its literal sense and *tzedakah* in a spiritual sense, as mentioned, regarding the dissemination of traditional Judaism wherever one’s influence reaches. I would be pleased to receive good news regarding the arrangements for all of the above.

Regarding the beginning of your letter concerning your place of work, which you say is the reason you have not written until now, etc.—it is a matter of wonder and surprise, for you are working among our fellow Jews, and all of Israel are responsible for one another. Who knows—perhaps the intent of your work there is so that through you, there will be an increase in the "candle of a *mitzvah* and the light of Torah," and the "light of Torah," which is the inner dimension of Torah that has been revealed in our generation through the teachings of Chassidus in its light and vitality. If even in years past there was no Jew who did not have a moment or a point through which one could influence him, how much more so in these days, when in the environment about which you write and in which you work, there is a visible awakening toward the true sanctities of the Children of Israel—namely, the Torah and the tradition of our fathers, as well as study (which leads to actual deed, the fulfillment of practical *mitzvos*). Regarding such things, our Sages have said: "There is nothing that can stand before the will."

With blessings for good tidings and to be inscribed and sealed for good.

P.S.
It was a pleasure for me to read the few lines regarding your brother, the G-d-fearing man, etc., may he live and be well, who is succeeding in the activities of spreading the light of Torah in an ever-expanding environment, and especially among the youth, the boys and girls, may they live, who are the builders of the future and its foundation.

## 7,334

B"H, 22 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn

To my dear friend, the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and learned, occupied with communal needs,
R' Shmuel Menachem Mendel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letters from 27 Menachem Av and 3 Elul, in which you write regarding what is transpiring among Anash in the Holy City of Jerusalem, may it be rebuilt and established. It appears that because you record these things some time later, several matters—some of which are surely important—did not make it into writing. Surely this will be rectified in the future; it is possible to jot down main points and markers on the spot, and fill them in during your leisure time, and so on.

By the way, regarding your letter of 27 Menachem Av, the content of what was discussed here during the farbrengen—that the revelation of the knowledge of Matan Torah was merely a matter of an addition—is obviously baseless from the outset, for Matan Torah is the totality of the order of all Seder Hishtalshelus, etc., etc. There is no need to elaborate on **the** most simple matter. What was actually discussed is that although ten constitute a *parheisya* (public gathering) that fills the entire world—meaning, it fills the breadth of the surface area—this is not the case regarding depth and height, which is an *hemshech* from a most lofty place; in this regard, there are many levels even far above ten. It is a simple matter that these levels are infinite in height and depth. This is the concept of *shes she-revavah* (six hundred thousand)—the "Wise One of Secrets" who draws down the level of *raz* (secret). And similarly regarding Matan Torah. It seems that several matters are recorded in this vein, and it is astonishing how the reader does not sense at all what is being said—which indicates a total lack of attention. And to the wise, a word is sufficient...

With blessings for good tidings and to be inscribed and sealed for good,

**M. Schneerson**

In response to your question—it is correct that it is registered in the Chabad housing project, and naturally, you should continue and complete it. As for the type of apartment—consult with your friends on the spot and decide, together with your wife, may she live—for success.

## 7330

B.H., 26 Elul, 5720
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 13/9, in which you outline the chapters of your life and your recent decision to study in a yeshiva for a year, and you mention a doubt that has arisen in your mind regarding your livelihood and what will be after you leave the yeshiva.

It is not clear from your letter whether you are truly troubled by the thought that these specific months you will spend studying in yeshiva are the very things that will disrupt your livelihood! On the contrary, one should hope that after you taste the flavor of studying our Torah—Torah of life—without other studies that cause confusion, you will see that it is good, true goodness, and not only regarding the life of the World to Come, but also for the life of this world. For we see tangibly that the most essential matter upon which a person’s happiness depends is the feeling that he does not stand in life isolated (himself on one side, and the entire world and environment on the other). The negation of this point among those who calculate their world is only through faith in the Creator of the world, who leads it and supervises it even now—in the terminology of our Sages, "below ten handbreadths." If in all eras this matter was essential for a person’s peace of mind, all the more so in our era, after the destructions and decrees, when our only hold and standing in life is pure and strong faith. It is obvious that faith will not be strengthened by learning a profession or trade, or even by studying science; on the contrary, when one engages in sciences as they are in our current era, they undermine all foundations [and even more so, they undermine the foundations upon which the so-called exact sciences are built]. This is not the place to enter into proofs for all this, but rather to emphasize that the first condition for a proper livelihood is a person’s peace of mind and his ability to concentrate on matters of livelihood. Furthermore, for one who is a person of intellect—of whom it is said, "he who increases knowledge increases pain" at the sight of what is happening in his environment—it is impossible to base and establish his worldview more and more except by dedicating his time and being in an environment permeated with the worldview of our Torah, the Torah of life, and connected with deepening the study of Torah (and as mentioned above, without distractions from other studies). After the foundation is laid in a strong and firm manner as mentioned, then one should endeavor to establish the "building" of livelihood by learning a profession or the like. In other words, the months and years that you will dedicate solely to the study of Torah are what will establish your livelihood in the future.

Regarding your question of how you will earn a living after some time—since you are at an age where you are likely to discover in yourself a change in your aspirations regarding what your livelihood will be, and especially after you dedicate your time for a certain period in the manner described—it is not worthwhile to decide this now (regarding your future work). It will be sufficient for this matter at its proper time, i.e., when the time comes to be concerned with it **in practice.**

The conclusion from what has been said is that you should study in the yeshiva, which is entirely dedicated to the study of our holy Torah, for a certain period of time—and the more one increases this, the more established he is in life. After this, or close to the end of this period, you will concern yourself with matters of livelihood. May it be the will of Hashem, who supervises each and every one with Divine Providence, that He guide you in what is truly good before Him.

With blessings for good news and to be inscribed and sealed for good,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

P.S.
My hope is that you have fixed times for the study of the inner dimension of the Torah, which in our generation has been revealed in the teachings of Chassidus. It is this that provides an added vitality to the life of a Jewish person in this world—vitality from the side of goodness and purity—even though winds that are not appropriate blow in the street, and the wise will understand. It would be good to study a lesson in this Torah every day, with an addition on the holy Shabbos, which is holy to Hashem.

## 7338

By the Grace of G-d,
Days of Selichos, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Mr. Eliezer Aryeh Halevi, may he live,
Known as Prof. Finkelstein

Shalom u’Bracha,

I received your letter in due time. Although, due to the multitude of my preoccupations, I did not reply in writing, it is understood that I am following the developments and changes in the institution you head, and primarily the state of mind of the students in the institution.

This brings me to emphasize once again the point I have spoken about on several occasions, and I believe also in my letters to you: that as far as can be determined, there has never been a more opportune time than in our days to arouse the youth and young people to accept the yoke of Torah and mitzvos in the literal sense—that is, to fulfill the mitzvos in daily life, the practical mitzvos in actual performance. Every moment, every opportunity, and every possibility that is not utilized for this properly is a loss that cannot be recovered.

It is understood that I do not mean by this that through preaching moral lessons and arousal regarding the above, the listeners will immediately turn into observers of the entire Torah and all 613 mitzvos. But certainly, as experience has proven, these things will act not only as a theoretical shift, but also as a preparation of the soul for practical change, as mentioned above, in the fulfillment of mitzvos—for the deed is the essential thing.

It is regrettable that precisely those who are in the position of leaders and influences—who by the nature of things are of an older generation than the listeners and students, and whose spiritual character was formed twenty or thirty years ago—still dwell in a fear that may have had a place then. This is based on the maxim, "If you grasp too much, you grasp nothing," and a hair’s breadth seems to them like a mountain and like grasping too much. It is difficult to convince them, as they hold the claim of yiras shamayim, saying that if they arouse the youth and listeners in the manner mentioned at the beginning of my letter, they might frighten the listeners, and the entire connection with them will be lost, and who knows to what depths they might descend.

I have written all the above openly, because precisely on this point, according to what I have heard, you are not gripped by the aforementioned fear. Yet, it is certain to me that you are not utilizing **all** the power of your influence in the aforementioned direction [perhaps due to opposition from those of the middle generation about whom I wrote above. And even this apprehension, if I may ask your forgiveness, has no basis in reality, because as mentioned above, the listeners and students will certainly be on your side. After all, in the United States in particular, the fathers are increasingly listening to the children, and the teachers to the students].

Incidentally, this is also one of the signs of *ikvesa d’Meshicha*, the final generation of the galus—the generation before the beginning of the Geulah. As the prophet foretold, "And he shall turn the heart of the fathers to the children"—which Rashi explains as "through the children." And although initially this is the opposite of a blessing, as the Tosefta at the end of Sotah states, "Youths will shame the elders, a son will degrade a father, etc."—we have been commanded to turn darkness into light and bitterness into sweetness.

Regarding what you wrote about the value of study and *iyun*, etc.: while it is a decided *halacha* that "study is great," the reason is immediately emphasized: because it leads to action. Furthermore, in order for the study to succeed and be established in the soul, it is necessary that it not be in contradiction—at least not in blatant contradiction—to the daily life of those engaged in the study, both the teacher and the student. Moreover, according to the explanation of the nature of the soul of the Jewish people in the teachings of Chassidus, the preparation of the soul to absorb the words of our Torah—which is a holy Torah and at the same time a *Toras Chaim* (Torah of life)—is precisely through daily life that conforms to its instructions. This is a fundamental difference between our Torah—even though it is said of it, "For it is your wisdom and your understanding in the eyes of the nations"—and other wisdoms and sciences. In the study of wisdom, even the wisdom of ethics, it does not contradict its understanding even if the student conducts himself in practice in complete opposition to the aforementioned ethics. This is not the case in the study of the wisdom of the Torah, not only the moral part of it but even in the laws of *tumah* and *taharah* and the like. As all this is emphasized in the short saying of our Sages: "And G-d was with him, for the *halacha* is like him in every place." For only by the fact that "G-d was with him" is the *halacha* directed, and in the phrasing of the Mishnah, by his "fear of Heaven preceding his wisdom."

All the above is said according to the approach explained in your letter regarding the necessity of study coming first. But according to my own approach, even without this, the emphasis on action is necessary, for this is the tool to influence the youth and young people with a constant, immediate impact, which they can then utilize also for persistence in their studies.

It is surely known to you that this year is the bicentennial of the *histalkus-hilula* of our master, the Baal Shem Tov. In connection with this, we have published the *Sefer Baal Shem Tov*, which has been sent to you separately.

And although I do not know your attitude toward the matters of the teachings of Chassidus, I am confident that you will find in the book much of interest, and perhaps also additional explanation and substantiation for what was written above.

With respect and blessing for good tidings, and with the blessing of *kesiva v’chasima tova* for a good and sweet year.

## 7339

B"H, Yemei HaSlichos, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

I received your letter in due time—a review of Kfar Chabad—and I ask your forgiveness for not having replied until now. One of the primary reasons for this is that I wanted to investigate and inquire into the entire matter in its details, and in such cases, face-to-face discussion is the most excellent form of investigation. Therefore, knowing that some of the heads of the village administration were due to be here, I wished to wait for a response until then.

It is with pleasure that I note that since you wrote your aforementioned letter, and according to the information I have received from the administration, several improvements have been made regarding the matters you wrote about. Surely you are already aware of them, perhaps even in greater detail.

I must respond to a few points you touched upon, for understandable reasons, with the preface that I am certain that had the author of the review based it on a visit he had made himself—knowing his attitude of friendship and goodwill toward the village and its affairs—he would have viewed things favorably, and his letter would have been written in a different spirit than one reviewed through the eyes of another.

I wish to emphasize two points in particular:

a) You surely know, perhaps better than I, about the change in attitude toward agriculture that has occurred throughout Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established), the tendency to diminish its value, and the movement to settle elsewhere. This change has been demonstrated most prominently of late in the announcements and speeches of Minister Mr. Dayan—and not merely a noticeable change, but a most sharp one; in the style of Chazal, he has "turned the bowl upside down." In our current period, if the high offices are speaking about this openly, it is certain that the change in the situation occurred quite some time ago, and a word to the wise is sufficient.

b) Furthermore, I must respond to your writing that in such-and-such a village the situation is better, even though it was founded at the same time, etc., and that there they succeeded in establishing and stabilizing, etc. The brief answer to this is that if you investigate how many people have left the village you write about as having succeeded and being established, and how long the people there have been there, and the proportion of people who arrived there recently—and compare this with the number of families who have left Kfar Chabad over the 12 years of its existence (which, as far as I know, is only one family throughout all these 12 years)—then it seems to me that this is the only place in all of Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established) where such a thing is found. If so, it requires great study as to which village has succeeded more in its stabilization, etc. For understandable reasons, I do not wish to elaborate on this, especially since I am a party to the matter.

In closing, as in the opening, my hope is strong that you will soon visit Kfar Chabad yourself. Consistent with your character and friendship, you will view the matters and their details with a favorable eye, and it will be a pleasure for me to receive a review from you after this visit.

Approaching the New Year, which is coming upon us and all of Yisrael for good and for blessing, I wish you and all that is yours a *Kesivah V'Chasimah Tovah* for a good and sweet year, both in your general affairs and in your personal affairs, in both material and spiritual matters alike.

With respect and blessing.

## 7440

B"H, Days of Selichos, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Mr. Yitzchok Koren

Shalom u’Bracha!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter dated the 17th of Elul and the one preceding it, and I am certain that you have also received my letter in due course.

Regarding what you wrote concerning the implementation of a public committee, etc., and that because I did not reply to you on this matter, you proceeded with the actions, etc.—the essence of the matter is that when there is confidence that there is someone to rely upon, it is sometimes better that things be done on their own initiative rather than through external prompting. Especially, as mentioned, when there is a sense of confidence that what is straight and good will be done.

I was pleased to receive the news regarding the laying of the cornerstone, as well as the photographs, and in this, your participation, etc., is also included. It is my hope that this will be a good beginning for a good continuation, increasing and progressing, adding more light. In truth, all of a person’s actions are in the nature of a beginning, since our Torah is eternal, our people are eternal, and all traditional values are eternal. Therefore, no matter how much a limited human being may act and achieve, it is still disproportionate to the Ein Sof.

It is understood that I am not coming to deliver a sermon, but rather to find merit for myself for repeating and emphasizing the same point several times. The reason is that I see here a matter that concerns generations and generations of generations, and every improvement and rectification of an institution founded on the purity of holiness in general, and of a Yeshiva institution in particular, brings about tremendous results and fruits and fruits of fruits.

Approaching the New Year, which is coming upon us and upon all of Israel for good and for blessing, I wish you and all that is yours a good and sweet year, both in your general affairs and in your private matters.

With blessings for a *Kesivah V’chasimah Tovah* for a good and sweet year, in both material and spiritual matters alike, and with respect.

## 771

B"H, Days of Selichos, 5720
Brooklyn, N.Y.
The Bicentennial Year of the passing of the Baal Shem Tov, of blessed memory.

To the sons and daughters of Israel, wherever they may be,
May Hashem protect them and grant them life,

Peace and blessings!

The days of Selichos at the conclusion of the year, and the days of the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah at the beginning of the year, underscore the great concept of **Teshuvah**, with which the year ends and with which the year begins.

Teshuvah, as is known, contains two primary parts: **Charatah al he-avar** — an internal regret over the past, and **Kabbalah al le-haba** — a deep resolve for the future.

However, the ultimate purpose of Teshuvah is **Teshuvah** — returning to the previous state — **she-yehei le-ratzon lifnei Hashem u-merutzeh ve-chaviv lefanav yisbarach kemo kodem ha-chet** (to be accepted and beloved by the Ribono shel Olam just as one was before the sin).

This means, firstly, that Teshuvah must be carried out in a way that leaves no impression on the **future**, not only in one’s actions and conduct (**ma'aseh**) but also in one’s speech and thought (**dibbur u-machashavah**).

Secondly — and here lies the innovation of Teshuvah — Teshuvah removes all stains from the **past** as well. Although the past is beyond the domain of man, the Ribono shel Olam,

Who is entirely outside the category of *zman* (time), and therefore *hayah, hoveh, ve-yihyeh* (past, present, and future) are unified in a single moment,

Has bestowed a special power and property upon Teshuvah, such that through Teshuvah, a person becomes the "master" over the past as well; he can change the **past** itself. And not only can he neutralize it, but he can also rectify it and transform it into good, as Chazal say: **Zedonos na'asu lo ke-zechuyos** (intentional sins are transformed for him into merits).

* * *

This power of Teshuvah — through which a person is given mastery over the past — is possible because, on one hand, as stated above, Teshuvah draws its strength from a "source" that is higher than time.

On the other hand, a Yid must utilize this power in a way that permeates his entire existence, penetrating and reaching the part of his neshamah that is also higher than time and its changes — **the penimiyus ha-nefesh** and **etzem ha-nefesh**, the nefesh elokis (the Divine soul) which is **tamid** (constantly) *be-emunah ito* (faithful to Him — to the Ribono shel Olam), because it is *chelek Eloka mi-ma'al mamash* (a literal part of G-dliness).

- It is on the basis of this bond between a Yid and the Creator that the Alter Rebbe, the Baal HaTanya ve-haShulchan Aruch, expressed that a Yid neither wants to be, nor can be, severed from Elokus (G-dliness).

- And so the Ribono shel Olam says, *Ahavti eschem amar Hashem* (I have loved you, says Hashem), and nothing can change that.

* * *

And just as Teshuvah encompasses and permeates the person entirely — from his etzem ha-nefesh to his conduct in daily, mundane life — so too, *middah ke-neged middah* (measure for measure), it brings the Yid to the highest and deepest level — *hu mudbak ba-Shechinah, ahuv ve-nechmad karov ve-yadid* (he becomes attached to the Ribono shel Olam, beloved by Him), leading to a *kesivah va-chasimah tovah* for a good and sweet year, with children, life, and ample sustenance, in all the needs of daily life.

With the blessing of a *kesivah va-chasimah tovah* for a good and sweet year, in both gashmius and ruchnius alike,

**Menachem Schneerson**

## 7742

*[Late 5720]*

...Regarding the time for removing tefillin — it is understood that the most correct way is as written in *Hayom Yom*.

Regarding your question about completing the three words in *Krias Shema* by means of the *shaliach tzibbur* saying "Kel Melech Ne'eman" — for the time being, I have not found an explicit instruction on this. However, in my opinion, it is simple: a) he is fulfilling the obligation for his entire congregation, and b) even the one who has not yet finished *Krias Shema* is part of his congregation, and he is supplemented with those three words while he is saying *Krias Shema*.

#### ***7742***

The letter is to R' Shalom Rivkin, and was written in the margins of his letter dated Wednesday, Parshas Nitzavim, 5720.

Additional letters to him — see above 7732, and the references in the notes there.

In *Hayom Yom*: 19 Menachem Av.

**Regarding completing the three words in Krias Shema:** When one prays with a *tzibbur* but does not finish the three paragraphs with the *shaliach tzibbur*, does the fact that the *shaliach tzibbur* repeats them count for him as well?

## 7443

*[Late 5720]*

...Regarding what you wrote in your letter about the reason you stopped studying Tanya: it is understood that this is most astonishing, for regarding all parts of our Torah it is said, "If one says, 'I have not labored but I have found,' do not believe him," and why should it be any wonder that the aforementioned study is included in this? And as for your writing that you did not understand a single thing, that is a great exaggeration. Rather, as is usual with many, you surely began to study in the reverse order [for in your type of study, the aforementioned learning should begin with Iggeres HaTeshuvah, then Part II of Tanya, and then Part I]. In any event, you should do as mentioned now, or it would be more appropriate that after you study Iggeres HaTeshuvah, you study Kuntres U'Maayon. With effort, you will understand it even when studying by yourself, and you can obtain it either in Lod or at Tzeirei Agudas Chabad. However, it is understood and also simple that the first time one studies, one does not scrutinize every single word, but rather grasps the general concept, and only afterwards does one scrutinize it more deeply, similar to the way one studies Gemara. May it be the will [of Hashem] that you do as stated, and may the Ribono shel Olam grant you success.

## 7444

[Summer 5720]

...It is obvious that one cannot find someone to travel there except by speaking with those who are suitable for this. A single conversation is not enough. Furthermore, a conversation just for the sake of "fulfilling an obligation" is insufficient, as is the case with all matters of Torah and mitzvos.

Likewise, it is necessary to arrange for someone else to speak and persuade them, and also to influence **those** who are hindering this.

Certainly, the "Melach" [the Rebbe's secretariat] will **assist** in this and will also speak — but the primary conversation must come from someone who comes from... and will explain the details and speak with warmth, etc., and afterwards the Melach will add to it and strengthen their words, etc.

The two couples you write about — each of them is suitable.

In response to what they told you, that I need to send [a request] — it is not my custom to be the one to initiate a conversation about this (except with those whom I am certain are connected, and in exceptional cases). And if **in truth** this is the only obstacle, then surely they will begin to speak and ask me, and then I will tell them my opinion on the matter.

#### ***7444***

Published in Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 23, p. 541.

## Z'tameh

[Year 5720]

... Based on what is written in the Rambam at the end of Hilchos Lulav, and this must be [applied] throughout the entire day — based on the ruling in Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim 231, **"In all your ways know Him."** And the necessity for this is explained in Tanya, Chapter 26 and onwards, and in many places there.

* * *

Very, very appropriate (as explained in Likutei Torah, Shelach, regarding the concept of "very" [appearing] twice).

* * *

38, 3. That the "high, high" [level] of tefillah should also effect [a change] in the "low, low" [realm] (the act of eating, drinking, etc., "in all your ways").

#### ***Z'tameh***

**Based on what is written in the Rambam:** A response to the question: How can I bring myself to be in a state of simcha?

**Very, very appropriate:** A response to the question of whether to change the nusach of tefillah to the nusach of the Arizal.

**38, 3:** A response to a question regarding the aforementioned answer: I have studied the aforementioned [Likutei Torah, Shelach], but I have not yet been able to understand the intention of the Rebbe Shlita [the connection between it and the nusach of tefillah].
